To solve this problem, we need to extract all URIs from the given LLM response text. The solution involves identifying URIs that typically start with schemes like `http`, `https`, or `ftp`, and formatting them into a list where each URI appears on a separate line. Any non-URI text should be removed. If no URIs are found, the response should be an empty space.

### Approach
1. **Regular Expression Matching**: Use a regular expression to identify URIs in the text. The regex pattern `r'https?://\S+'` matches URIs starting with `http` or `https` and continues until the next whitespace character. This captures contiguous strings that resemble URIs.
2. **Splitting and Cleaning**: For each matched URI, split the string by commas to handle cases where URIs are separated by commas. Strip whitespace from each part and check if it starts with a valid scheme (`http://`, `https://`, or `ftp://`).
3. **Trimming Trailing Characters**: Remove any trailing non-URI characters (like punctuation) from each cleaned URI part to ensure only the URI remains.
4. **Collecting Valid URIs**: Collect all cleaned and valid URI parts into a list. If no valid URIs are found, output an empty space.

### Solution Code
```python
import re

def format_uris(response_text):
    if not response_text.strip():
        return " "
    
    pattern = re.compile(r'https?://\S+')
    matches = pattern.findall(response_text)
    uris = []
    
    for match in matches:
        parts = match.split(',')
        for part in parts:
            part = part.strip()
            if part.startswith(('http://', 'https://', 'ftp://')):
                while part and part[-1] in ',.!?;:"\'()':
                    part = part[:-1]
                if part:
                    uris.append(part)
    
    if not uris:
        return " "
    else:
        return "\n".join(uris)
```

### Explanation
1. **Regular Expression Matching**: The regex pattern `r'https?://\S+'` efficiently scans the input text to find any contiguous sequences starting with `http` or `https` followed by any non-whitespace characters. This ensures that we capture potential URIs.
2. **Splitting and Cleaning**: Each matched URI is split by commas to handle comma-separated URIs. Each resulting part is stripped of leading and trailing whitespace. Only parts starting with valid schemes are considered.
3. **Trimming Trailing Characters**: After verifying the start of each part, trailing non-URI characters (like commas, periods, etc.) are removed to ensure the URI is clean and valid.
4. **Collecting Results**: Valid URIs are collected into a list. If the list is empty, an empty space is returned. Otherwise, the URIs are joined into a single string with each URI on a new line.

This approach efficiently processes the input text to extract and format URIs as required, handling common cases such as commas, whitespace, and trailing punctuation to ensure the output is clean and standardized.

 Basic DOM-based detection often struggles to capture relationship-specific, non-structural semantics. A more promising direction involves leveraging knowledge graphs (e.g., W ikidata, UMLS) to represent relationships between entity mentions, allowing for more robust cross-sentence and cross-document inference. Additionally, methods that incorporate discourse analysis or discourse parsing (e.g., Rhetorical Structure Theory, PDTB) can explicitly capture connective phrases and implicit relations.

Machine learning approaches can benefit from incorporating discourse information and knowledge graph embeddings. However, there are several challenges:

1.  **Data Scarcity**: High-quality, annotated data specifically for relation detection is expensive and time-consuming to create, particularly for specific domains like medical or legal.
2.  **Computational Cost**: Semantic role labeling, discourse parsing, and knowledge graph alignment can be computationally expensive, limiting their applicability in large-scale or real-time systems.
3.  **Evaluation Metrics**: Standard metrics like precision and recall may not fully capture the nuances of relationship detection, particularly for implicit or multi-hop relationships.

Future work should focus on developing more robust, resource-efficient, and generalizable methods for relation detection, including:

*   **Multimodal Integration**: Incorporating visual and other modalities to enhance entity and relationship understanding, particularly for scientific publications.
*   **Few-Shot Learning**: Developing models that can generalize to new relationships and domains with minimal training data.
*   **Cross-lingual Relation Detection**: Extending methods to work effectively across different languages.

The field needs to move beyond simple pattern matching and structure recognition to develop a deeper understanding of the semantic relationships that exist between entities.
